But for most of us, the day never comes.   Christi McCully, a Creative Memories consultant, teaches people how to turn a box full of photos into a colorful scrapbook that will last several lifetimes.   The Hewitt resident said she became interested in the craft after she discovered that all of her childhood memories were lost forever.   ``I have always been into pictures and when I moved away from home, I left several scrapbooks behind and my parents stuffed them into a storage shed,'' she said.   ``After I recovered them, I was horrified. There was no air conditioning in the shed and the albums were ruined. The pictures and pages were beyond repair.''   Creative Memories is a national company that employs consultants such as McCully to teach people how to preserve their photos using acid-free paper, stickers and albums.   Humidity and photo albums are the worst enemies to photos, said Douglas Severson, a conservator at the Art Institute of Chicago.   ``In some albums, photographs are ruined much more quickly than they would be if you just left them in a shoe box,'' he said.   The worst type of album, he believes, is the most common one: the so-called magnetic album. It has no magnets, but its cardboard pages grip photographs on a sticky adhesive coating covered by a layer of plastic that is peeled back to position the photos.   In such albums, the cheap-quality cardboard gives off peroxides that cause yellow staining in the whites of the prints in both black and white and color photos.   McCully, who recently moved to the area from Jacksonville, Fla., believes she is the only consultant in Waco. People who see her beautiful albums usually become discouraged because they look so professional.   However, if you can use a pair of scissors and peal off stickers, you can make a creative photo album, said McCully, who offers classes to groups and individuals in her home and at seminars.   She offers the following tips to help people create albums safe for their photos and appealing to the eye:   -- The most important thing that she emphasizes is to crop, or trim the dead space, from your photographs using scissors.   ``Most people look at my album and say, ``What a great photographer you are,''' she said. ``But it's not me, it's the cropping that makes the photos look professional and fun.''   -- McCully also recommends using colorful, acid-free paper to frame pictures on a page and to write documentation. She said documentation is important because no matter how visible the event is to you right now, you will eventually forget it.   ``If you don't write a caption for the picture, an important piece of history is lost forever,'' she said.   -- Use photo-safe adhesives to fasten pictures.   -- Record details with colorful water-proof pens. Write humorous captions and list names of those in the picture.   -- Add creative touches such as stickers, vacation brochures and ticket stubs.   -- Store albums in an upright position and not on top of each other.   -- Store negatives in a fire-proof box or a safe deposit box. .  </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19940721.0071 type=story > <HEADLINE> INLINE SKATING MAKES FOR A GREAT WORKOUT </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> DAYTON, Ohio  (BC-INLINE-SKATES-COX) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_199407.gz"

(NYT5) JOHANNESBURG, South Africa -- Dec 12, 1995 -- SAFRICA-WHITEFLIGHT, 12-11 -- At the end of this month, Heidi Moller, her daughter Melissa, 5, and their trained attack dogs Max, right, and Misha will move from South Africa to Germany. (Selwyn Tait/New York Times Photo)  (NYT4) JAMESTOWN, N.D. -- Dec. 12, 1995 -- FARMER-LONELY-HEARTS, 12-11 -- An epidemic of bachelorhood is striking rural America, leading some men and a few women, to advertise for suitable mates. David Gasal, a fourth-generation farmer is among those finding an absence of eligible women. He says he likes farm life, ``I just wish I had somebody to share it with.'' Eric Hylden/New York Times Photo)  (NYT3) ILO, Peru -- Dec. 12, 1995 -- PERU-COPPER-POLLUTE, 12-11 -- Anna Jilapa stuffs cloth in the cracks of her doors and windows to prevent the acrid smoke released by the Southern Peru Corp.'s plant from entering her home. Her 1-year-old son, Brian, suffers from severe respiratory problems and often coughs up thick mucous. She blames the smelter. The plant has been accused of being one of the world's biggest polluters, pouring 2,000 tons of sulfur dioxide into the air each day. The company denies it has been the cause of any health problems.(Vera Lentz/New York Times Photo)  (NYT2) NEW YORK -- Dec. 12, 1995 -- FILM-FASHION, 12-11 -- Jean Paul Gaultier holding a corset he designed for The Octopus, the Siamese- twin rulers in ``The City of Lost Children.'' With a $4.2 billion budget and immense influence over the flow of research dollars, the Institute has an enormous impact in the shaping of public opinions. (Marty Katz/New York Times Photo)  N.Y. (Jose R. Lopez/New York Times Photo)  (NYT12) LOS ANGELES -- July 16, 1996 -- HEART-TRANSPLANT-HEALTH-CLR, 7-16 -- James Taylor, 72, received a repaired heart four years ago. An unusual program at the Medical Center of the University of California at Los Angeles gives elderly heart patients and younger patients with medical complications an alternative to imminent death: they can get transplants using hearts that are considered to have a higher-than-usual chance of failing and that would otherwise be discarded. (Michael L. Tweed/New York Times Photo)  (NYT11) MARIETTA, Ga. -- July 16, 1996 -- OLY-COMM-COX-CLR, 7-16 -- A shortage of commuter buses in Marietta causes people to wait as long as 2 hours Monday morning. Commuters in the suburbs of Atlanta are being forced to find alternative forms of transportation since downtown parking meters are turned off and a ban on curbside parking is in effect through the end of the Games on Aug. 4. (Joe McTyre/Atlanta Journal-Constitution Photo)  (NYT10) FORT BENNING, Ga. -- July 16, 1996 -- OLY-BENNING-COX-CLR, 7-16 -- The Army has produced half of the nation's 38 Olympic shooting medals. (Gregory Rec/Boston Globe Photo)  (NYT6) NEW YORK -- July 16, 1996 -- NY-ALFRESCO-TRAVEL-2-CLR, 7-16 -- Leisurely drinks among the trees at the Bryant Park Cafe in midtown. (Ed Quinn/New York Times Photo)  (NYT3) WASHINGTON -- July 16, 1996 -- NATION'S-TABLE-1-CLR, 7-16 -- Raku, Mark Miller's new diner in Washington's Dupont Circle, serves Asian street foods to a lively crowd of diners. ``It's not about a meal at Raku,'' says Miller. ``It's about a place to hang out with friends.'' The first two, he believes, are La Potiniere in Gullane, Scotland, and the White Horse Inn in Chilgrove, Sussex. (Jonathan Player/New York Times Photo)  N.Y. (ATTENTION EDITORS: This article is for use by clients of The New York Times Syndicate's DISCOVER NEWS SERVICE. For more information about purchasing this article as a ``separate-buy'' or becoming a client of DISCOVER NEWS SERVICE, contact one of the New York Times Syndicate sales representatives listed at the end of the text.) (ART ADV: Color art is available on Wieck, Newscom or by calling The New York Times Syndicate's Graphics desk at 212-499-3339.) c.2001 Discover magazine Compiled by THE EDITORS OF DISCOVER MAGAZINE (Distributed by New York Times Special Features)  COULD SOUND HAVE SAVED PRINCESS DI?  When Princess Diana's limo crashed in 1997, she might have lived if paramedics could have swiftly stopped the massive internal bleeding in her chest, lungs and head. Now a group of scientists at the University of Washington in Seattle has developed an experimental technology that could rapidly seal off an accident victim's damaged blood vessels without the need for a single incision.  Doctors commonly stop hemorrhaging with powerful electrical currents that shrink blood vessels and induce blood clots, but the technique, called electrocautery, works only for wounds close to the surface. High-intensity ultrasound _ waves having frequencies of 1 to 10 megahertz, far above the highest audible sounds _ offers far more flexibility, claims bioengineer Shahram Vaezy. His team has found a way to focus very-high-energy ultrasound at precise sites deep within an injured area.  The ultrasound beam is absorbed by the tissue and converted to intense, localized heat, which stops the bleeding by cauterizing the wound.  ``No other energy mode _ not X rays, not lasers _ can go deep and be brought into a tight focus,'' he says.  SEX, DRUGS AND PMS  While studying the ways cocaine constricts the blood vessels in the brain, Harvard pharmacologist Marc Kaufman noticed that female addicts generally did better in rehabilitation therapy and suffered less neural damage than did males with comparable habits. The reason, he believes, is that the women may be benefiting from hormonal changes associated with their menstrual cycle.  Kaufman took MRI brain scans of men and of a group of women at different phases of their menstrual cycle, after giving them the equivalent of two lines of cocaine. Men suffered a 20 percent drop in brain blood flow, while women at the beginning of their cycle showed no change. Even women in later menstrual stages had only a 10 percent decline.  Kaufman believes estrogen's ability to relax blood vessels may lead to a hormone therapy for both male and female cocaine users.  DID THE ORACLE INHALE?  To the ancient Greeks, the oracle at Delphi was the voice of Apollo. To Jelle de Boer, the oracle was more likely an ordinary woman high on hydrocarbons.  De Boer, a geologist at Wesleyan University in Connecticut, believes the medium who served in the shrine delivered her prophecies while immersed in a trance induced by a naturally occurring mixture of ethane, methane and ethylene, a sweet-smelling gas that was used as an anesthetic in the early 20th century.  De Boer had discovered that the Temple of Apollo lies atop the crossroads of two geologic faults. The slipping and expansion of these faults, he believes, allowed volatile gases to escape from oil-rich limestone deposits and rise through fissures and springs into the sunken base of the shrine.  When de Boer and his colleagues recently analyzed sediments from inside the shrine's foundation, they discovered trapped molecules of ethane and methane. They also found these gases, as well as ethylene, bubbling up in the Kerna Spring, northwest of the temple.  Nearly 2,000 years ago, the author and temple priest Plutarch fingered a sweet-smelling gas as the source of the oracle's predictions. But the French archaeologists who excavated the site a century ago dismissed the theory as myth.  De Boer thinks it is time to give Plutarch proper credit: ``He was a very good naturalist. He gave what is basically a primitive geological report.''  SCIENCE SURFING  _ Take Care of the Beasts (www.anc.org): The Animal News Center, created by two ASPCA members, covers political and scientific developments relating to wildlife, pets, farm animals and lab animals.  _ Explore the Seas (www.oceanexplorer.noaa.gov): The National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration brings oceanography to life with beautiful maps and photos, along with information on past and present expeditions.  _ Search Out Real Research (www.scirus.com): Plug in keywords for any scientific topic and this resourceful search engine calls up links to related sites, papers and journal articles (some of which cost money).  _ Get a Jump On Winter (snowcrystals.net): It's always snowing on this Caltech Web site, which includes a stunning gallery of snowflake images along with information about how plain water can take on so many exotic forms. The stories are available as ONESHOTS. For purchase information, see end of text.  ALL THE ART IS FREE OF CHARGE AND AVAILABLE ELECTRONICALLY.  THE INNER SAVANT  THE-INNER-SAVANT-DISCOVER _ Nadia appeared healthy at birth, but by the time she was 2, her parents knew something was amiss. She avoided eye contact and didn't respond when her mother smiled or cooed. At 6 months, she spent hours in repetitive play, such as tearing paper into strips. But at 3 1/2, she picked up a pen and created from memory sketches of galloping horses that only a trained adult could equal. Nadia is an autistic savant, a rare condition marked not only by severe mental and social deficits but also by a mysterious talent that appears spontaneously _ usually before age 6. Allan Snyder, a researcher and physicist, has advanced a new explanation of such talents. Savants, he believes, can tap into the human mind's remarkable processing abilities. A report. By Douglas Fox.  FREE ART:  _ One color slide shows Dustin Hoffman and Tom Cruise in a scene from the 1988 movie ``Rain Man.'' The individual Hoffman drew upon for his character was named Joseph, by far one of the most famous savants. Joseph could immediately answer this question: ``What number times what number gives 1,234,567,890?'' His answer was ``Nine times 137,174,210.''  THE GREATEST UNANSWERED QUESTIONS OF PHYSICS  UNLOCKING-SCIENCE-SECRETS-DISCOVER _ About two years ago, NASA administrator Daniel Goldin suggested that a special report be funded to detail how much astronomers and physicists can benefit from one another's insight. Recently, the National Research Council's committee on the physics of the universe released that report. It details profound questions, some of which are presented in this article, that if answered could unlock the secrets of existence and deliver a new age of science within several decades. By Eric Haseltine.  FREE ART:  _ One color slide shows a star-filled night sky.  Both stories are also available in Spanish. THE INNER SAVANT  THE-INNER-SAVANT-DISCOVER _ Nadia appeared healthy at birth, but by the time she was 2, her parents knew something was amiss. She avoided eye contact and didn't respond when her mother smiled or cooed. At 6 months, she spent hours in repetitive play, such as tearing paper into strips. But at 3 1/2, she picked up a pen and created from memory sketches of galloping horses that only a trained adult could equal. Nadia is an autistic savant, a rare condition marked not only by severe mental and social deficits but also by a mysterious talent that appears spontaneously _ usually before age 6. Allan Snyder, a researcher and physicist, has advanced a new explanation of such talents. Savants, he believes, can tap into the human mind's remarkable processing abilities. A report. By Douglas Fox.  FREE ART:  _ One color slide shows Dustin Hoffman and Tom Cruise in a scene from the 1988 movie ``Rain Man.'' The individual Hoffman drew upon for his character was named Joseph, by far one of the most famous savants. Joseph could immediately answer this question: ``What number times what number gives 1,234,567,890?'' His answer was ``Nine times 137,174,210.''  THE GREATEST UNANSWERED QUESTIONS OF PHYSICS  UNLOCKING-SCIENCE-SECRETS-DISCOVER _ About two years ago, NASA administrator Daniel Goldin suggested that a special report be funded to detail how much astronomers and physicists can benefit from one another's insight. Recently, the National Research Council's committee on the physics of the universe released that report. It details profound questions, some of which are presented in this article, that if answered could unlock the secrets of existence and deliver a new age of science within several decades. By Eric Haseltine.  FREE ART:  _ One color slide shows a star-filled night sky.  Both stories are also available in Spanish. "111390"	"he"	"believes"	" As for that ``too many notes'' reference, it takes place after the performance of Mozart's early opera ``Seduction From the Seraglio,'' when the Emperor Joseph II (wonderfully played by Jeffrey Jones) tells the composer what is wrong with the piece. ``Just cut a few, and it will be perfect,'' he advises. ``Which few did you have in mind, Majesty?'' responds a sarcastic Mozart. No, there aren't too many notes here. Even a longer ``Amadeus'' feels and looks just right. --- AMADEUS (4 stars) (R: brief nudity) -Starring: F. NATIONAL (a)  CHURCH-CENTERS-ART (Undated) _ For decades before the case of defrocked priest John Geoghan elevated clergy sexual abuse into a national crisis, the Catholic Church was spending millions of dollars to quietly treat accused sex offenders in a constellation of psychiatric hospitals _ some independent, some church-affiliated _ advertised in the back pages of religious publications. By Ellen Barry. 3000 words.  OVARY-TEST (Undated) _ Boston researchers have identified a test for identifying ovarian tumors in their earliest stages - a potentially life-saving advance that could be the female equivalent to prostate-cancer screening in men. Ovarian cancer is among the deadliest cancers in the United States, partly because it's so difficult to detect in its early stages. By Stephen Smith. 800 words. By Douglas Belkin. 760 words.  LEHIGH-COLUMN (Undated) _ Eds: Scot Lehigh is writing on a local topic. His column will not move. We are at war and city services are strapped, but nothing stops Americans from getting their ``stuff'' from Whale-Mart. By Derrick Jackson. (Visit the Star-Telegram's online services on the World Wide Web: www.star-telegram.com)  N A T I O N A L / T E X A S (''a'' category unless otherwise noted)  CASH-SEIZED  WEATHERFORD, Texas _ John Forrest smelled something rotten, and it wasn't just the spoiled food in the back of the truck. Forrest, a Texas Department of Public Safety trooper, was responsible for the seizure of $718,000 in suspicious cash during the stop of a semi-trailer rig with a trailer full of rotten french fries and spinach in Parker County on Tuesday. The cash, found under a bed in the sleeper cab, is among the most taken by DPS troopers, said DPS spokeswoman Tela Mange. The record is $2 million, she said. By Bill Teeter  LOST-SUPERINTENDENT  ARLINGTON, Texas _ One of the ``best'' discoveries of the school district's centennial has been the finding of a lost superintendent. Historians have long agreed that H. Tarpley deserves credit for overseeing the birth and growth of the Arlington school district because he was its first superintendent. He wasn't. According to early school board minutes, he was the second. Arlington's first superintendent and principal was a man named J.N. Johnston. Administrators rediscovered Johnston while researching early board minutes for the district's 100th anniversary celebration. By Matt C. Frazier  CHENEY-PITCH  ARLINGTON, Texas _ Vice President Dick Cheney is popping out of his undisclosed location long enough to throw out the first pitch at the Rangers' home opener Friday at The Ballpark in Arlington. Cheney will be the first vice president to make the pitch in franchise history. Presidents Gerald Ford and George Bush (the elder) each threw the ceremonial first pitch while in office. Rangers owner Tom Hicks invited the vice president a couple of months ago. Cheney's press office notified the Rangers on Tuesday that he would take part in the ceremony. By Sean Wood  FAKE-CLOTHES  UNDATED: They call them the Oakley investigators. They travel throughout Texas, Florida and Oklahoma. Their target: counterfeit clothes. Monty Drake and Associates Inc. of Fort Worth is one of the few private investigation agencies in the state that track down retailers and manufacturers of counterfeit clothes. They're hired by such giants as Nike, Adidas, Tommy Hilfiger, Abercrombie and Fitch and Oakley. Story about the agency and the investigators who chase down the clothes bad guys. By Domingo Ramirez Jr.  AIR-MARSHALS  UNDATED: Some U.S. Border Agents and immigration inspectors are trading their badges for the undercover clothes of federal air marshals, stirring concern among immigration experts who say the losses will hurt the U.S. Immigration and Naturalization Service. By Diane Smith  PROPERTY-TAX  AUSTIN, Texas _ Acting Lt. Gov. Bill Ratliff is expected to unveil a new school finance plan on Wednesday that would rely on a statewide property tax to equalize education funding between poor and rich districts. Ratliff's proposal would replace the formula dubbed ``Robin Hood.'' By R.A. Dyer  TECH-PRESIDENT  UNDATED: Texas Tech University releases its list of finalists to replace chancellor John Montford. By Rebeca Rodriguez  C O M M E N T A R Y  COLUMN-SANDERS  UNDATED: As the tearful woman joined her granddaughter on the stage of an eighth-grade graduation last year, her proud face looked so familiar. It was Halloween night, 1986, and I had seen her through the glass door of the John Peter Smith Hospital emergency room. Her son, the third-oldest of nine children, had been shot and was in surgery. The PMs news desk, 212-556-1927, opens at 7 a.m. Eastern Time. To get story repeats before this hour call 212-621-1595 for reruns on AP DataFeatures or 214-980-8305 or 8367 for repeats on UPI DataNews.  NYT News Service  INTERNATIONAL _ (Moved in ``i'' category)  MIDEAST-HAMAS (Gaza City) _ Interviews with four of the five principal leaders of Hamas show a self-satisfied group. The leaders said they regard the suicide attacks as the most successful strategy they have ever employed. By Joel Brinkley.  NATIONAL _ GENERAL (Moved in ``a'' category)  SCI-COUSINS-MARRY (Undated) _ Genetic counselors say that despite numerous state laws and widespread belief that first cousins should not marry, the risks are not high. For the first time, the counselors have issued guidelines and say the laws should be abolished. By Denise Grady.  (ART ADV: Graphic is being sent to NYT graphic clients.)  SCI-KIDS-CANCER (Undated) _ In an echo of the mammogram findings of last year, researchers say screening tests can detect neuroblastoma, one of the most common childhood cancers, but there is no evidence that early detection does anything to lower death rates. By Gina Kolata.  BUSINESS DAY _ (Moved in ``f'' category)  ADELPHIA-MARKETPLACE (Undated) _ Many investors may have been surprised by the recent revelations from Adelphia Communications, the nation's sixth-largest cable television company, whose shares fell an additional 16 percent on Wednesday as the bad news mounted. But last week's disclosure _ that the Rigas family, which controls the company, had borrowed at least $2.3 billion from Adelphia without recording that fact on the books _ should not have come as a shock. By Florence Fabricant.  (ART ADV: Graphic is being sent to NYT graphic clients.)  ENRON-ASSETS (Washington) _ A provision of state law in Texas and Florida could allow several former executives of Enron who own luxury homes to shield millions of dollars in assets from angry shareholders and former employees, a prospect that has alarmed members of Congress who want to close down the sunbelt loophole. By Philip Shenon.  INEQUALITY-ECONSCENE (Undated) _ Nearly 50 years ago, Simon Kuznets, a winner of the Nobel in economic science, predicted that income inequality would follow an inverted-U pattern over time _ ``widening in the early phases of economic growth when the transition from the preindustrial to the industrial society was most rapid; becoming stabilized for a while; and then narrowing in the later phases.'' New research that updates and extends this classic work, however, turns the Kuznets curve on its head. By Alan B. Krueger.  (ART ADV: Graphic is being sent to NYT graphic clients.)  SHACKLETON-AD-COLUMN (Undated) _ While weather can be mundane, it can also be dramatic, and in that vein two cable television networks are joining forces to promote special, related programs on both channels this month. The unusual joint venture involves the A&AMP;E Network, which is presenting ``Shackleton,'' about a famous expedition to the South Pole in 1914, and the Weather Channel, which is presenting ``StormWeek,'' about severe weather conditions. By Allison Fass.  COMMENTARY (Moved in ``k'' category)  HERBERT-COLUMN (Undated) _ Commentary by Bob Herbert.  SAFIRE-COLUMN (Undated) _ Commentary by William Safire.  ENTERTAINMENT (Moved in ``e'' category)  MADISON-COUNTY-SEQUEL (Undated) _ Ten years after his ``Bridges of Madison County'' conquered the best-seller list (it sold 6 million hardcover copies in North America, which makes it the best-selling hardcover novel of all time), Robert James Waller has written what he describes as an epilogue to the original story. That book, ``A Thousand Country Roads,'' will appear in stores late this month. Despite the platinum pedigree of ``Country Roads,'' it makes its way to print with a surprising number of questions, commercial, artistic and otherwise. By Leslie Kaufman.  GRAMMYS (New York) _ The Grammy Awards will again be held in New York City. By Jon Pareles.  BOOK-EDITORS (New York) _ One of the enduringly intriguing questions that pops up every now and then within the book publishing community is how does one calculate the value of the book editor? It was raised again by the scramble among publishers to sign up the second literary novel by the mega-selling author of ``Cold Mountain.'' As of this writing, the sale has not been made, delayed in part by the author's quandary over this very question. By Martin Arnold.  FRANCE-ALGERIAN-ACTRESS (Paris) _ For an actress of 25 who graduated from the National Conservatory of Dramatic Art in June, Rachida Brakni's screen and stage career has begun on an impressive note. Yet she has been noticed for more than her acting and striking appearance. She is also the French-born daughter of poor Algerian immigrants. By Alan Riding.  TV-NEWS (Undated) _ Critic's Notebook: The recent coverage of the Arab-Israeli conflict suggests that network newscasts in the United States remain important by default. Their audience remains huge. More than 30 million people watch the network news programs each night, while the cable news channels _ CNN, MSNBC and Fox News Channel _ combined would be lucky to reach 3 million in prime time. By Caryn James.  LIVING-BOOK-REVIEW (Undated) _ ``Living It Up,'' by James B. Twitchell, reviewed by Janet Maslin.  TV-BATTLE-REVIEW (Undated) _ Watching ``Battle for the Holy Land'' may not give you a better understanding of the horror there. It surely won't give you hope. But the program, Thursday's installment of the ``Frontline'' series on PBS, does put a human face on the intractable conflict, even if that face is seen in shadow or mostly wrapped in a scarf. By Ron Wertheimer.  HOME (Moved in ``l'' category)  ANIMAL-DESIGN-SECRETS (Berkeley, Calif.) _ A Q&AMP;A with professor Robert Full, a biologist who studies the way animals move and spends his days handling geckos, poisonous centipedes and other multilegged creatures. Creepy crawlers, he believes, are a source of wisdom for anyone who is designing robots, like Rhex, the all-terrain Hummer of mechanical movers. Fuller answered questions about the promising convergence of biology and design. Chee Pearlman.  HIGH-TECH-REVISED (New York) _ Joan Kron's apartment, with its bathroom-tiled living-room seating and chemical-flask flower vases, was widely publicized in the 1970s when Kron was the co-author of ``High Tech,'' a domestic guide to industrial resources like commercial-kitchen shelving. The book became a bible of style. This month, Kron is completing the renovation of her three-bedroom apartment on the Upper East Side. ``I didn't want to be frozen in time,'' she said, discussing design generally. By William L. Hamilton.  JUNGLES-DOMESTICATED (Miami) _ When you have a name like Raymond Jungles, maybe you are fated to rip up lawns to bring the jungle back to South Florida. These are invented jungles, to be sure, with rare palms from Madagascar, African mahogany trees, Asian bamboo. By Anne Raver.  GARDEN-Q&AMP;A (Undated) _ Garden questions answered. By Leslie Land.  CIRCUITS (Moved in ``a'' category)  CIR-INTERACTIVE-TV (Undated) _ After more than 20 years of effort, interactive TV has still failed to catch on in the U.S. Is this the moment? By Jennifer 8. Lee.  CIR-EDISON-LAB (West Orange, N.J.) _ A visit to Edison's labs in New Jersey, looking for lessons in innovation. By John Schwartz.  CIR-ONLINE-SEARCHES (Undated) _ New search engine efforts that aim to out-Google Google. By Lisa Guernsey.  CIR-GOVT-ONLINE (Undated) _ Update on government services online. By Rebecca Fairley Raney.  CIR-GAMES (Undated) _ Jet Set Radio Future and State of Emergency. By Charles Herold.  CIR-PORTABLE-MUSIC (Undated) _ Over the last 18 months a new class of slightly larger MP3 players has emerged with their own hard drives that can hold as many as 8,000 songs. You can easily carry around the equivalent of an entire music collection without taking a single CD out of the living room. By Wilson Rothman.  CIR-SENSORS (Berkeley, Calif.) _ Organic transistors: cheap sensors for monitoring food inventory and freshness. By David L. Margulius.  CIR-CIRCUITS-COLUMN (Undated) _ The weekly column: Home networking modules that plug into your electrical wiring. By David Pogue.  (ART ADV: Graphic is being sent to NYT graphic clients.)  CIR-GEEK-NOTES (Undated) _ Techno news and notes: Linux-based PDA; wireless Bluetooth U.S.B. connector for Macs; Targus thumb-scanner security device for laptops; Logitech fabric keyboard for palms; an Estari laptop that folds like a book, providing two screens.  CIR-ONLINE-SHOPPING (Undated) _ The weekly feature: Car buying, part 2. By Michelle Slatalla.  CIR-Q&AMP;A (Undated) _ Techno questions answered: Why Windows slows over time, even with good maintenance; using new programs on Mac OS X; restoring toolbars in Microsoft Word. By J.D. Biersdorfer. Reporting on nature, science and exploration in the National Geographic Society tradition, stories from NATIONAL GEOGRAPHIC NEWS SERVICE will be transmitted by wire on Mondays, Wednesdays and Fridays.  The 700-word articles will be filed in the ``a'' (general news) category code and will be transmitted with the following slugs: BC-NATGEO-TODAY-KEYWORD-DATE-NYTSF or BC-NATGEO-NEWS-KEYWORD-DATE-NYTSF.  For purchase information, see end of text.  TRANSMITTED SEPT. 20, 2002  WINEMAKERS TOAST THE DRY SPELL  NATGEO-TODAY-WINE _ Virginia winemaker Dennis Horton's Viognier grapes have produced a bumper crop _ thanks, he believes, to the summer's parching drought. In 11 states, this year has been the driest or second driest on record, capping four years of inadequate rainfall. This summer has been the hottest in the United States since the 1930s, and the third hottest on record. But, for many grape varieties, that kind of weather is good to grow in. By Peter Standring. WITH PHOTO.  ART AVAILABLE: One color image _ of September grapes on the vine _ is available electronically by calling The New York Times Syndicate's Graphics Desk at 212-499-3339.)  TRANSMITTED SEPT. 18, 2002  WISDOM FROM THE OWL  NATGEO-TODAY-LEARNING _ The barn owl is a source of wisdom about how humans learn, especially as we age. The older we get, the tougher it is to pick up new skills like languages, music and mathematics. But a new study of juvenile and adult barn owls suggests that older birds learn more, and faster, when their training goes step by step. The barn owl study has lessons for human victims of brain injury who must relearn skills _ and for general teaching practices as well. By Bijal P. Trivedi. WITH PHOTO.  ART AVAILABLE: One color image _ of Stanford researchers Eric Knudsen and Brie Linkenhoker, with a barn owl _ is available electronically by calling The New York Times Syndicate's Graphics Desk at 212-499-3339.)  TRANSMITTED SEPT. 16, 2002  THE BIRDS AND THE BEES  NATGEO-NEWS-REPRODUCTION _ For all species, the secret to being an evolutionary success is making lots of babies that survive to become adults, which in turn reproduce. The strategies of mating are astonishingly diverse, says Olivia Judson, an evolutionary biologist at Imperial College in London. Judson takes on a Miss Lonelyhearts persona as Dr. Tatiana to explain, in thoroughly researched detail, the hows and whys of the birds and the bees in her new book, ``Dr. Tatiana's Sex Advice to All Creation.'' By Hillary Mayell. WITH PHOTO. Baghdad tea.   </P> </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20021012.0102 type=advis > <HEADLINE> Carter's strength is plainly Georgia </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> ATLANTA  (BC-CARTER-EDITORIAL-1013) </DATELINE> <TEXT> As the world acknowledges Jimmy Carter's Nobel Peace Prize, Georgians have the right to feel proud of their favorite son. After all, the principles that permeate Carter's life are rooted in the South Georgia Sunday school lessons and rocking-chair porches of Plains _ population, 637.  That Carter and his wife, Rosalynn, could live by those simple, eloquent principles and still rise to the pinnacles of power sends a powerful message: Winning the respect of the peoples of the world does not require sacrificing the public good to arrogance, big money, sloganeering or power for power's sake.  Carter was not always hailed for strong leadership when he was the state's governor or the nation's president. His great strength as a humanitarian was finally recognized in his post-presidential contributions through the Carter Center.  His hands-on style shaped the center into an effective voice for peaceful change, whether Carter was trudging through the mud villages of Nigeria to eradicate Guinea worm, sweating in Atlanta's summer sun with a Habitat for Humanity house building crew, monitoring a local election in rural China or mediating with intractable leaders in Haiti and North Korea to avert disaster. Carter's Camp David Accords remain a high point in Middle East negotiations at a time when the United States has lost its credibility as an honest broker in that part of the world.  At the heart of Carter's greatness is a religious and moral structure seldom seen in world leaders, one he does not foist on others, one he lives. He has always contended that ``the most important things in life are the things you don't see, the things that never change.'' What has made Carter an effective peacemaker is that he sees some redeeming quality in every human being, even the most despicable.  In one of his poems, ``Always a Reckoning,'' Carter writes how things on the family farm had to balance out _ acres plowed and cotton picked. Humans are also capable of that kind of reckoning, he believes, if they are open to fair-minded give and take. It is a message that other leaders should take to heart, especially those who would resort to violence first and seek peace later.  Carter the peacemaker is a deserving winner of the Nobel prize. But most of all, he is one of us.  Martha Ezzard is a member of The Atlanta Journal-Constitution editorial board. TRANSMITTED Dec. 6, 2002  IN THE SOUTH PACIFIC, A DANGEROUS LEAP FOR TOURIST DOLLARS  NATGEO-SOUTH-PACIFIC-RITUAL (Pentecost Island, Vanuatu) _ It's a death-defying ritual, practiced for centuries. On this South Pacific island, in the former New Hebrides, the Naghol, translated as ``land-diving,'' is an ancient form of bungee jumping _ a rite of passage and a way to bless the yam crop. Divers ascend flimsy wooden towers 75 feet or higher, tie jungle vines to their ankles and leap. Divers aim to skim the ground with the top of their heads. Not always do they come away unharmed. These days the islanders are finding other reasons to dive. Tourists have discovered the Naghol, and on Pentecost Island, a fever to cash in has taken hold. By Zoltan Istvan. WITH PHOTOS. And here the news may be good: By many accounts, even where inequality is increasing, poverty is on the decline. </P> "	"nyt_eng_200301.gz"
"135869"	"she"	"believes"	" Dalton says that Roosevelt's image is so fixed in the American consciousness that almost anyone today equates his image with robust health, glorious military action, uncompromising patriotism and the great outdoors. This, she believes, is partially myth, with much of the exaggeration attributed to Roosevelt himself. He made wonderful copy, and he knew it. He was ``dee-lighted'' to cooperate with journalists and biographers who spent a disproportionate number of words on the colorful aspects of his character. Later writers often accepted the ``facts'' of earlier books and articles, and perpetuated the image. Dalton's self-imposed challenge was to bore through to the reality of the man. </P> "	"nyt_eng_200301.gz"

He has always kept a Bible in the gym and insisted on three rules for his fighters: no earrings, no cursing and no fighting outside the ring. </P> "	"nyt_eng_200301.gz"
"136282"	"he"	"believes"	" (Visit the Star-Telegram's online services on the World Wide Web: www.star-telegram.com) nn </P> </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20030110.0186 type=advis > <HEADLINE> THE PREACHER AND THE EDITOR </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> 01-10-03 18:37:00EST (BC-MLK-ESSAY-CLARK-1000-) </DATELINE> <TEXT> On May 10, 1967, Martin Luther King Jr. wrote a personal letter, unpublished until now, to Gene Patterson, editor of The Atlanta Constitution.  Patterson had just won a Pulitzer Prize for his columns supporting racial justice and civil rights. King, who had won a Nobel Peace Prize three years earlier, was magnanimous in his praise: ``I am sure that all people of good will in general and all Atlantans in particular are very proud of you and your magnificent achievements.''  The correspondence between the black preacher and the white editor, which surfaced during preparation of a new book on journalism and civil rights, serves as a mirror and a model for our times: a mirror in the way it reflects on war, justice and race, and a model of two men conversing across racial and political boundaries in search of mutual understanding.  Patterson wrote a column that anchored the editorial page of the Constitution every day for nine years, from 1960 to 1968. Continuing the white Southern liberalism espoused by his mentor and predecessor, Ralph McGill, Patterson used the drumbeat of his daily column to persuade his white Southern kinfolk that they must change on matters of race.  With respect, King was wrong about ``all Atlantans'' being proud of Patterson's Pulitzer. The editor's family members were harassed as ``nigger lovers.'' For security, he kept a ball-peen hammer near his typewriter.  But Patterson was emboldened by the early leaders of the struggle for black equality. To read his columns _ more than 3,200 in all _ is to relive the daily human and political drama that we now call the civil rights movement.  On Aug. 28, 1963, Patterson was standing at the Lincoln Memorial, notebook in hand, recording the historic cadences of King's speech before 200,000 marchers.  ``In a few impassioned and triumphant moments,'' he wrote, ``King swept the marchers to a new vision of the Negro's destiny in America by praising and celebrating America, and lifting their eyes from the 'valley of despair' to purple mountain majesties. 'I have a dream,' he boomed again and again, and each dream showed him liberty and pursuit of happiness for all races of Americans soon, from the cliffs of the Rockies to the slopes of the Alleghenies, from Stone Mountain in Georgia to the broad Mississippi. 'I have a dream,' he roared, weeping, and his dream stretched from sea to shining sea, and all the way from the speaker's stand at the Lincoln Memorial to the far end of a crowd that stretched to the Washington Monument.''  By 1966 young black leaders like Julian Bond had begun turning their attention to the war in Vietnam, equating oppression of the poor in America with military oppression of the Vietnamese people. Patterson, who had fought in Patton's army in World War II and reported from the front lines in Vietnam, found this new view of American foreign policy offensive, and said so in columns critical of King.  King's letter to Patterson addresses the difference between the two men: ``I did not come to my position on the war in Vietnam without great soul-searching and long moments of reflection and meditation. I have come to the conclusion that the ultimate measure of a man is not where he finds himself in moments of comfort and moments of convenience, but where he finds himself in moments of challenge and moments of controversy.''  With his letter King sent a pamphlet he had written on Vietnam, along with an autographed copy of his new book, ``Where Do We Go From Here,'' and invited Patterson to meet for an off-the-record conversation on war and civil rights. Patterson responded, ``Just as you know my difference with you is a sincere one, I want you to know that I consider your position also to be the expression of a clear heart.''  Patterson did not tell King that, on two occasions, the FBI had approached the editor with information designed to embarrass the preacher. Go down to this airport, said one agent, and you'll catch King with a woman who's not his wife.  Patterson later described to Howell Raines, now executive editor of The New York Times, what he told the FBI man before he showed him the door: ``Look, we're not a peephole journal. Furthermore, I'm shocked that you would be spying on an American citizen, whether it's Dr. King or some other person, because if it can happen to him, it can happen to all of us.''  The proposed meeting between King and Patterson never took place. Within a year King was dead, felled by an assassin's bullet. Two days later, on April 6, 1968, Patterson wrote, ``Martin Luther King Jr. is home now, forever. If his life and death failed to move us to take our own worst natures in hand to build here one nation in brotherhood because we believe in it, then he failed. He could do no more in his 39 years than show us the way.''  On the day of King's funeral, Patterson pressed through the crowds and was ushered in the back door of Ebenezer Baptist Church. He wrote, ``We will not even now see the overwhelming injustice we continue to visit upon these people who still believe in us unless Dr. King's death teaches us that we must hereafter be among them, and know them, and take their hands and walk with them as men whose friendship will ennoble us. Jobs, housing, education are only programs. Knowing and loving our neighbors is the needed memorial to Dr. King.''  At the age of 79, Patterson continues to write and speak about war, race and justice. He has lived long enough to see the American system of apartheid dismantled, the city of Atlanta transformed and thriving, and an African-American woman, Cynthia Tucker, writing from his old desk at the Constitution. In spite of such progress, this is not the promised land.  ``The work is not finished,'' he wrote in 2002. Only brave political and editorial leadership, he believes, will ``lift up the people we held down for so long and cleanse from American history the stain of past wrongs.''  The final paragraph of King's letter continues to speak to Patterson, as one great Georgia writer expresses himself to another with no expectation that his words will be published some 35 years later:  ``It is my great hope that the dark clouds of war will soon pass away and the deep fog of misunderstanding will be lifted from our fear-drenched world, and in some not too distant tomorrow the radiant stars of peace and brotherhood will shine over our great nation and the world. Sincerely, Martin Luther King Jr.''  (Author and journalist Roy Peter Clark is a senior scholar at the Poynter Institute in St. Petersburg, Fla. He is co-editor of the new book ``The Changing South of Gene Patterson: Journalism and Civil Rights, 1960-1968.'')  -----  EDITORS: To publish this article, it must be purchased. The NYT frontpage advisory, with layout description, will move by 7:30 p.m. ET. The night supervisor for The New York Times News Service is Pat Ryan; phone: 888-346-9867 or 212-556-1927; e-mail: patryan(at)nytimes.com.  KOREA-U.S. (Washington) _ The United States on Friday condemned North Korea's decision to withdraw from the nuclear non-proliferation treaty, saying it further escalated the crisis and posed a serious challenge to global disarmament efforts. Washington also rejected Pyongyang's contentions that it could immediately withdraw from the treaty and that the step was legal and justified by steps taken by the United States and the International Atomic Energy Agency. Early Friday, President Bush called China's president, Jiang Zemin, to urge joint action to stop North Korea from what several senior administration officials believe is an effort to accelerate a program that would develop five or six nuclear weapons in the next few months, while the White House focuses on Iraq. By David E. Sanger with Julia Preston.  Editors, KOREA-U.S.-1STLD-WRITETHRU has moved and will be updated; this story subsumes KOREA-U.N. budgeted earlier and now scratched.  And  KOREA-ENVOYS (Santa Fe, N.M.) _ Clinton-era diplomatic big-gun Bill Richardson launched a second round of talks with two North Korean envoys early Friday as the crisis over Pyongyang's nuclear program escalated, officials said. The former U.N. ambassador was upbeat over the tone of the private talks, which cast him as an unusual go-between in the deepening standoff over the Stalinist hermit state's weapons scheme. By Steven R. Weisman.  And  KOREA-ASSESS (Washington) _ North Korea's decision to withdraw from the Nuclear Non-proliferation Treaty is a calculated exercise in brinkmanship that appears intended to step up the pressures on the Bush administration to engage in full-fledged economic and political negotiations with Pyongyang. In a sense, North Korea's threat to withdraw does not change the basic situation on the Korean peninsula. Pyongyang has been violating the treaty for years by engaging in a clandestine program to enrich uranium. It has already thrown out the agency's inspectors and removed key monitoring equipment. By Michael R. Gordon.  JOBS-REPORT (Undated) _ The nation continued to bleed tens of thousands of jobs in December, the Labor Department reported Friday, jolting forecasters who had expected a modest upturn in employment and suggesting that American business remains highly pessimistic about the economic future. Payrolls in non-farm businesses dropped by 101,000, and the unemployment rate stayed at 6 percent. The Labor Department also revised the number of jobs lost in November to 88,000 from 40,000. Though cornerstones of economic growth like increasing productivity and financial stability remain intact, companies have been unwilling to expand production or commit to investments in new equipment and technology that would support job creation. By Daniel Altman.  An early version has moved.  With photos, NYT2-3.  DEATH-PENALTY-DECLINE (Undated) _ For the first time in a generation, the number of inmates on death row has dropped. And the number of people sentenced to death in 2001, the most recent year for which data is available, was the lowest since 1973. Despite enduring and strong popular support for the death penalty, these numbers suggest that those directly involved in the justice system share at least some the concerns that prompted Gov. George Ryan of Illinois to undertake a sweeping review of his state's approach to capital punishment. The drop in the number of death sentences is the product of a number of phenomena, including a lower murder rate. But legal experts across the political spectrum agreed that advances in DNA technology, combined with recurring disclosures about sloppy or abusive police work have raised grave doubts about the way capital punishment is carried out. By Adam Liptak.  With graphic.  IRAQ-MOBILIZE (Hinesville, Ga.) _ At a surplus shop outside Fort Stewart, home of the Third Infantry Division, women are working until midnight in a mad dash to stitch names into uniforms. The town and base is crawling with activity. This is the fretful deployment dance before the big, long hush. And it's happening _ again. The Army's entire 3rd Infantry Division, based at Ft. Stewart, is leaving, and few corners of the country are turned as inside out by the showdown with Iraq as Hinesville, Ga. During the gulf war, after the 3rd Division was dispatched to Saudi Arabia, many businesses here went under. By Jeffrey Gettleman.  With map.  CALIF-FISCAL (Sacramento, Calif.) _ Gov. Gray Davis releases his budget plan calling for tax increases and major spending cuts to deal with a $34 billion deficit. By John M. Broder.  With graphic. (BC-IRISH-EGAN-BOS) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_200301.gz"
"136551"	"he"	"believes"	" ISABEL AMORIM SICHERLE in Brazil at 55-11-3023-3331; fax, 55-11-3022-2965; e-mail: sicheianytimes.com. </P> </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20030113.0205 type=advis > <HEADLINE> THE PREACHER AND THE EDITOR </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> 01-13-03 17:48:00EST (BC-MLK-ESSAY-CLARK-1000-) </DATELINE> <TEXT> On May 10, 1967, Martin Luther King Jr. wrote a personal letter, unpublished until now, to Gene Patterson, editor of The Atlanta Constitution.  Patterson had just won a Pulitzer Prize for his columns supporting racial justice and civil rights. King, who had won a Nobel Peace Prize three years earlier, was magnanimous in his praise: ``I am sure that all people of good will in general and all Atlantans in particular are very proud of you and your magnificent achievements.''  The correspondence between the black preacher and the white editor, which surfaced during preparation of a new book on journalism and civil rights, serves as a mirror and a model for our times: a mirror in the way it reflects on war, justice and race, and a model of two men conversing across racial and political boundaries in search of mutual understanding.  Patterson wrote a column that anchored the editorial page of the Constitution every day for nine years, from 1960 to 1968. Continuing the white Southern liberalism espoused by his mentor and predecessor, Ralph McGill, Patterson used the drumbeat of his daily column to persuade his white Southern kinfolk that they must change on matters of race.  With respect, King was wrong about ``all Atlantans'' being proud of Patterson's Pulitzer. The editor's family members were harassed as ``nigger lovers.'' For security, he kept a ball-peen hammer near his typewriter.  But Patterson was emboldened by the early leaders of the struggle for black equality. To read his columns _ more than 3,200 in all _ is to relive the daily human and political drama that we now call the civil rights movement.  On Aug. 28, 1963, Patterson was standing at the Lincoln Memorial, notebook in hand, recording the historic cadences of King's speech before 200,000 marchers.  ``In a few impassioned and triumphant moments,'' he wrote, ``King swept the marchers to a new vision of the Negro's destiny in America by praising and celebrating America, and lifting their eyes from the 'valley of despair' to purple mountain majesties. 'I have a dream,' he boomed again and again, and each dream showed him liberty and pursuit of happiness for all races of Americans soon, from the cliffs of the Rockies to the slopes of the Alleghenies, from Stone Mountain in Georgia to the broad Mississippi. 'I have a dream,' he roared, weeping, and his dream stretched from sea to shining sea, and all the way from the speaker's stand at the Lincoln Memorial to the far end of a crowd that stretched to the Washington Monument.''  By 1966 young black leaders like Julian Bond had begun turning their attention to the war in Vietnam, equating oppression of the poor in America with military oppression of the Vietnamese people. Patterson, who had fought in Patton's army in World War II and reported from the front lines in Vietnam, found this new view of American foreign policy offensive, and said so in columns critical of King.  King's letter to Patterson addresses the difference between the two men: ``I did not come to my position on the war in Vietnam without great soul-searching and long moments of reflection and meditation. I have come to the conclusion that the ultimate measure of a man is not where he finds himself in moments of comfort and moments of convenience, but where he finds himself in moments of challenge and moments of controversy.''  With his letter King sent a pamphlet he had written on Vietnam, along with an autographed copy of his new book, ``Where Do We Go From Here,'' and invited Patterson to meet for an off-the-record conversation on war and civil rights. Patterson responded, ``Just as you know my difference with you is a sincere one, I want you to know that I consider your position also to be the expression of a clear heart.''  Patterson did not tell King that, on two occasions, the FBI had approached the editor with information designed to embarrass the preacher. Go down to this airport, said one agent, and you'll catch King with a woman who's not his wife.  Patterson later described to Howell Raines, now executive editor of The New York Times, what he told the FBI man before he showed him the door: ``Look, we're not a peephole journal. Furthermore, I'm shocked that you would be spying on an American citizen, whether it's Dr. King or some other person, because if it can happen to him, it can happen to all of us.''  The proposed meeting between King and Patterson never took place. Within a year King was dead, felled by an assassin's bullet. Two days later, on April 6, 1968, Patterson wrote, ``Martin Luther King Jr. is home now, forever. If his life and death failed to move us to take our own worst natures in hand to build here one nation in brotherhood because we believe in it, then he failed. He could do no more in his 39 years than show us the way.''  On the day of King's funeral, Patterson pressed through the crowds and was ushered in the back door of Ebenezer Baptist Church. He wrote, ``We will not even now see the overwhelming injustice we continue to visit upon these people who still believe in us unless Dr. King's death teaches us that we must hereafter be among them, and know them, and take their hands and walk with them as men whose friendship will ennoble us. Jobs, housing, education are only programs. Knowing and loving our neighbors is the needed memorial to Dr. King.''  At the age of 79, Patterson continues to write and speak about war, race and justice. He has lived long enough to see the American system of apartheid dismantled, the city of Atlanta transformed and thriving, and an African-American woman, Cynthia Tucker, writing from his old desk at the Constitution. In spite of such progress, this is not the promised land.  ``The work is not finished,'' he wrote in 2002. Only brave political and editorial leadership, he believes, will ``lift up the people we held down for so long and cleanse from American history the stain of past wrongs.''  The final paragraph of King's letter continues to speak to Patterson, as one great Georgia writer expresses himself to another with no expectation that his words will be published some 35 years later:  ``It is my great hope that the dark clouds of war will soon pass away and the deep fog of misunderstanding will be lifted from our fear-drenched world, and in some not too distant tomorrow the radiant stars of peace and brotherhood will shine over our great nation and the world. Sincerely, Martin Luther King Jr.''  (Author and journalist Roy Peter Clark is a senior scholar at the Poynter Institute in St. Petersburg, Fla. He is co-editor of the new book ``The Changing South of Gene Patterson: Journalism and Civil Rights, 1960-1968.'')  -----  EDITORS: To publish this article, it must be purchased. Photo and graphic notes will be added on Friday. If you have questions about stories, call (888) 346-9867 or (212) 556-1927. For questions about photos, call (888) 603-1036 or (212) 556-4204.  On the Cover  Editors: The cover stories will be budgeted on Friday. Stories under consideration are features on a malpractice attorney and Martha Stewart's impending return from federal prison.  Commentary  ECON-COLUMN (Undated) -- The weekly Economic View column: Though Social Security's fiscal direction has taken center stage in Washington of late, Medicare's future financing problems are likely to be much worse. President Bush has asserted that the Medicare Modernization Act, which he signed in 2003, would solve some of those problems -- the logic is irrefutable, he said two months ago. Yet the Congressional Budget Office expects the law to create just $28 billion in savings during the decade after its passage, while its prescription drug benefit will add more than $400 billion in costs. So, how can Medicare's ballooning costs be contained? One idea is to let people die earlier. By Daniel Altman.  EVERYBODYS-BUSINESS (Undated) -- The column: Your humble servant grew up as the son of two brilliant economists. I studied economics at Columbia University under some big names in economics. When I was in law school at Yale, I took classes in finance in the graduate school of economics under geniuses. None of these people, however, taught me to be a great investor. I have been a stockholder of Berkshire Hathaway, Warren Buffett's company, for more than 20 years, and I have done well with the stock. But even reading Buffett's famous annual letters to stockholders did not teach me to be a great investor. The fact is that I am not a great investor, at least in the sense that I can consistently or even often beat the broad market indexes in any meaningful way. But here comes the important part: Many other people aren't great investors, either. By Ben Stein.  KARMAZIN-SIRIUS (Undated) -- It can be crowded at the top. That is what Mel Karmazin found after CBS, of which he was chief executive, merged with Viacom in 2000. He agreed to stay on as president of Viacom under the combined company's CEO, Sumner M. Redstone; four rocky years later, Karmazin stepped down and cashed in his options. Last fall, Karmazin signed on as the chief executive of Sirius Satellite Radio, the second-largest (and only other) satellite radio broadcaster after XM. The move recalls his roots as the founder of Infinity Broadcasting, the radio group that popularized Howard Stern. Karmazin spoke recently about Stern, who will be joining Sirius in January, as well as Redstone and Sirius's business. Following are excerpts from the conversation. By Laura Rich. In Q&amp;A format.  FIGHTING-GERMS (Undated) -- More and more, the United States is a nation of germophobes. The nation's germ obsession should come as little surprise. The news has been full of health worries -- whether from the SARS epidemic, viral outbreaks on cruise ships or biological weapons threats. During commercial breaks, TV advertisers have done their part to sound the alarm about germs lurking in places as varied as your laundry and your mouth. Consumers now have their pick of germ-zapping products: from toothbrush sanitizers and air purifiers to antibacterial soaps, cutting boards, clothing, keyboards and even refrigerators. About 75 percent of Americans use at least six or more antimicrobial products regularly. Have consumers gone too far? Perhaps. By Amy Cortese.  WOMEN-PAY (New York) -- Do you think that Lawrence H. Summers, Harvard's president, stirred up a hornets' nest by suggesting that women's brains are not genetically wired for math or science? Wait until you hear Warren Farrell on the subject of women's pay. Sure, Farrell accepts that women, as a group, are paid less than men. But the way he sees it, using pay statistics to prove sex discrimination is akin to using the horizon to prove that the world is flat. Women, he believes, methodically engineer their own paltry pay. They choose psychically fulfilling jobs, like librarian or art historian, that attract enough applicants for the law of supply and demand to kick in and depress pay. They avoid well-paid but presumably risky work -- hence, the paucity of women flying planes. And they tend to put in fewer hours than men -- no small point, he says, because people who work 44 hours a week make almost twice as much as those who work 34 and are more likely to be promoted. An At Lunch feature by Claudia H. Deutsch.  Investing  INVEST-CASH (Undated) -- After suffering through years of derisively low interest rates, people with cash on their hands may finally be able to get a decent return for parking their money safely in bank accounts or in money market funds. Such savers may still have a hard time coming out ahead, after taxes and inflation, but the overwhelming consensus is that the rate increases that the Federal Reserve began last June will continue and that such investments will become more attractive. By Robert D. Hershey Jr.  INVEST-OVERSEAS (Undated) -- The Strategies column: Investors who look overseas to reduce the risks of holding stocks can find support in a new study. Contrary to recent objections that this strategy does not work well, if it works at all, the research has found that global portfolios have been significantly less risky over the long run than ones containing domestic stocks alone. By Mark Hulbert.  CARS-VOLVO-COMPACTS (Undated) -- Volvo's first attempt at a line of small cars did not go well, but it is back with a new S40 (and a wagon version, the V50). Built on a well-regarded platform shared with the Mazda 3 and a new European version of the Ford Focus, the latest Volvos are compelling compacts. And, significantly, they give Volvo a footprint in a market that is expected to become very crowded in the next few years: Premium compact cars priced from roughly $25,000 to $33,000. Audi is about to bring its A3 to America, and both BMW and Mercedes have small cars on the way. The question remains whether Americans will learn to equate small with upscale? By Sheryl Jensen.  For Monday release:  CARS-RACECARS-COLLECT (Undated) -- Among the most valuable collector cars are racing machines that claim a piece of motorsports history, including Ford GT40s that became legends at Le Mans in the 1960s, or the very first Ferraris. One New York investment banker has a garage full of these racetrack icons, and like some of his fellow collectors he actually drives them on the street -- with state registration papers, license plates, annual safety inspections and all. Of course, it doesn't hurt to have top-notch mechanics at your beck and call. By Nick Kurczewski. To improve his calorific intake and promote an efficient recovery of his strength, nutrition via the positioning of a nasal gastric tube has begun, said the Vatican spokesman, Joaquín Navarro-Valls. The announcement did not say exactly when the feedings had started. But the ailing pope appeared silently at the window of his chambers at 11 a.m. local time, without a feeding tube in place. For the first time, the Vatican seemed to damp down a bit its normally upbeat assessment of the pope's medical condition, now characterizing his convalescence as slow. By Elisabeth Rosenthal and Ian Fisher.  An early lede has moved.  IRAQ (Baghdad, Iraq) -- A car bomb exploded outside a primary school west of Baghdad Wednesday, killing a guard, as insurgents launched attacks on pilgrims traveling to Karbala for a Shiite religious festival. The bomb detonated in the town of Abu Ghraib outside the Abu Ghraib School, Interior Ministry officials said. Five civilians standing nearby were also injured. By Robert F. Worth.  LAURA-BUSH (Kabul, Afghanistan) -- With Laura Bush on a five-hour visit to Afghanistan, with extraordinarily tight security. By Carlotta Gall.  WOLFOWITZ-EUROPE (Brussels, Belgium) -- Paul Wolfowitz came to Europe as a supplicant, trying to shed his image as a unilaterialist hawk and entreat his hosts to approve him as president of the World Bank. The nomination of Wolfowitz, the deputy secretary of defense and a leading architect of the American-led war in Iraq, to the World Bank post by President Bush has sparked a firestorm of protest among political commentators and in much of the European media. But instead of taking revenge and objecting to him, European leaders have given him the green light, however grudgingly, another sign of Europe's realization that it must repair relations with the United States in Bush's second term. By Elaine Sciolino.  ZIMBABWE-ELECT (Harare, Zimbabwe) -- Advancing Thursday's legislative elections in Zimbabwe. By Michael Wines.  INDONESIA-QUAKE ( Gunung Sitoli, Indonesia) -- A report on the earthquake from the island of Nias, off the western shore of Sumatra, where people are still lying under piles of rubble as their neighbors, with almost no heavy equipment, tear with their hands at blocks of concrete and piles of brick. By Seth Mydans.  KYRGYZSTAN (Bishkek, Kyrgyzstan) -- Developments. Felix Kulov, one of the two most popular Kyrgyz leaders to emerge from last week's coup, on Wednesday resigned as security chief, distancing himself from the new government just three months before a presidential election. His move raises concerns of more unrest and divisions among the impoverished central Asian state's new rulers, less than a week after President Askar Akayev fled. By Craig S. Smith.  CHINA-TAIWAN (Beijing) -- A high-ranking delegation from Taiwan arrives in Beijing. By Christopher Buckley.  UNITED STATES -- National General (a code)  MINN-SHOOTINGS (Dateline TK, Minn.) -- The investigation of a shooting spree that left nine people and the gunman dead on a Minnesota Indian reservation widens as the authorities seek to determine how many people may have plotted to commit the rampage. The authorities had said that 16-year-old Jeff Weise acted alone in killing nine people before taking his own life, but on Sunday they arrested, Louis Jourdain, a high school student and son of the Red Lake Band of Chippewa chief, as part of the investigation into a potentially wider plot. By Monica Davey.  SCHIAVO (Pinellas Park, Fla.) -- In a new legal twist in a case already marked by back-and-forth maneuverings, a federal appeals court agreed late Tuesday to consider a request for a new hearing on whether a feeding tube should be reconnected to the severely brain-damaged Terry Schiavo. The U.S. Court of Appeals for the 11th Circuit ruled without comment. The motion was being considered by all 11 judges on the Atlanta panel, a court official said Wednesday.  JACKSON-TRIAL (Santa Maria, Calif.) -- Prosecution testimony continues in the Michael Jackson child molestation trial. By Charlie LeDuff.  UNITED STATES -- Washington (w code)  SCOTUS-AGE-DISCRIM (Washington) -- The Supreme Court ruled Wednesday that older workers can, in some circumstances, recover damages from their employers for harm caused by age discrimination even if the harm was not deliberate. The court, ruling 5-3 in a case that has been closely watched by business interests, held that the 1967 Age Discrimination in Employment Act does allow such lawsuits. But the court also made clear that the estimated 75 million people covered by the law, meaning workers over the age of 40, must clear a high threshold of evidence to prevail. By Linda Greenhouse.  FINANCIAL (f code)  AIG-BERKSHIRE (New York) -- American International Group said it had discovered improper documentation for a controversial transaction with Berkshire Hathaway and said that it would delay the filing of its annual report with regulators. It was the first time that AIG has said a deal with General Re was improperly recorded as an insurance transaction. AIG also intends to seek relief from the SEC to allow the continued use of registration statements on Form S-3, which means it needs the agency's permission to continue selling registered life insurance products. By Gretchen Morgenson.  With  DUBLIN-INSURE (Undated) -- Dublin is the boom town in the growing use of finite insurance. Looking at how Ireland became the new Bermuda. By Timothy L. O'Brien and Brian Lavery.  MCI-QWEST (Undated) -- Qwest's board meets to discuss is options regarding MCI, including a counteroffer or taking its offer directly to shareholders. By Ken Belson and Andrew Ross Sorkin.  MITSUBISHI-SUIT (Tokyo) -- Mitsubishi Motors said it would sue seven of its former executives for damages, including three past presidents, for their roles in what it called a cover-up of vehicle defects that has decimated sales. By Todd Zaun.  ECONSCENE (Undated) -- The weekly Economic Scene column. By Alan B. Krueger. ENTERTAINMENT/ARTS (e unless otherwise indicated)  MCEWAN-NOVELS (New York) -- In London, where people study the literary pecking order more closely than Americans do, Ian McEwan is currently ranked the best British novelist of his generation. It is only a matter of time, he believes, before the mantle passes to Kazuo Ishiguro, who has a new novel just out, or to Julian Barnes or Salman Rushdie, who both have books coming later this year. But in fact McEwan, 56, has held the title at least since 2001, when his novel Atonement earned almost unanimously favorable reviews on both sides of the Atlantic, and the reception for his new book, Saturday, with just a few naysayers chiming in, has only cemented his standing. What his celebrity mostly means, McEwan said in an interview in New York this week, is that every time he has a book out, newspaper editors in Britain call him requesting, say, an essay on My Favorite Armchair or, as happened just recently, asking him to name his two favorite vegetables. By Charles McGrath.  SIN-CITY-FILM (Los Angeles) -- Robert Rodriguez's new film, Sin City, will be Harvey Weinstein's last release for Disney-owned Miramax Films and like Pulp Fiction and Kill Bill before it, pushes the limits of mainstream film violence. Cannibalism, decapitations, dismemberment and torture feature heavily in the story and Disney, which has been moving its mix toward the family end of the spectrum, faces the difficult task of squaring the circle without disassociating itself from what might be a big hit. By David M. Halbfinger.  TOWELHEAD-BOOK-REVIEW (Undated) -- Towelhead, by Alicia Erian (S&amp;S). Reviewed by Janet Maslin. NYT News Service  INTERNATIONAL  (Coded I)  ISRAEL-GAYS (Undated) -- When international gay rights leaders picked Jerusalem to hold a 10-day World Pride festival and parade this August, they said they wanted to make a statement about tolerance and diversity in the Holy City. Now conservative leaders of three faiths - Christianity, Judaism and Islam - have united to try to stop the festival from going forward. Their alliance was nurtured by a Christian pastor from San Diego who, like many American evangelical leaders, is an outspoken supporter of Israel and had formed relationships with Israeli politicians and rabbis. By Laurie Goodstein and Greg Myre.  With photos.  NATIONAL  (Coded a)  STATES-GAMBLE (Dover, Del.) -- Gambling has become a critical source of revenue for a number of states, in some cases surpassing traditional sources like the corporate income tax and helping states lower personal income or property taxes. We're drunk on gambling revenue, said Wayne Smith, the House majority leader in the Delaware Legislature. Gambling revenues are like free money. In Rhode Island, South Dakota, Louisiana and Oregon, not to mention Nevada, taxes from casinos, slot machines at racetracks and lotteries make up more than 10 percent of overall revenues, according to a new report. In Delaware, West Virginia, Indiana, Iowa and Mississippi, gambling revenues are fast approaching 10 percent. By Fox Butterfield.  With photo.  HITLER-PSYCHOLOGICAL-PROFILE (Undated) -- The first psychological profile of Hitler, commissioned by the Office of Strategic Services, a predecessor to the CIA, has been posted by the Cornell University Law Library on its Web site. By Benedict Carey.  FINANCIAL  (Coded f)  NY-STADIUM-DOLAN (Undated) -- If Cablevision loses its bid to block the Jets from building a stadium above the railyards on the Far West Side of Manhattan, which is likely to happen Thursday, its pugnacious chief executive, James L. Dolan, will be a double loser. Dolan will not only face the prospect of stiff new competition for Cablevision's Madison Square Garden, but he will have made Mayor Michael R. Bloomberg and much of the city's civic establishment his enemy. In addition, his unsuccessful campaign will have cost him a fortune, while he is still facing an internal battle at Cablevision with his own father, Charles F. Dolan, who controls the company. By Richard Sandomir, Geraldine Fabrikant and Charles V. Bagli.  CABLE-BULK-DEALS (Undated) -- Planned communities sprouting up across the Sun Belt and apartment blocks in big cities are the hottest battlegrounds for customers in the pay television market. With new subscribers harder to find, more consumers are being locked into compulsory plans in developments where a homeowners' association or management company charges residents for property upkeep, security and the like. By Ken Belson.  (Story moved on 3-29.)  AIG-MARKETPLACE (Undated) -- The accounting improprieties uncovered by American International Group and disclosed to the public Wednesday not only concede that the company's net worth will have to be reduced by $1.7 billion and that its annual financial report will be later than expected. They also paint an unattractive picture of a company that during the last five years, regularly reached into its bag of tricks to beautify its financial performance. By Gretchen Morgenson.  CIR-CIRCUITS-COLUMN (Undated) -- You might not imagine that there's a single movie-distribution channel left to invent. These days, a movie can reach you by pay-per-view, on a plane, at the video store, on a TV movie channel, on the Internet, at the front of a Greyhound bus, in the second row of a minivan or even -- get this -- in a movie theater. (Hey, it could happen.) Not to seem ungrateful, but none of these mechanisms deliver the holy grail: any movie, any time. There is, however, a low-tech distribution channel that comes pretty darned close: DVD rentals by mail. By David Pogue.  With graphic.  DAYLIGHT-AD-COLUMN (Undated) -- As most Americans prepare to push their clocks ahead this weekend when daylight saving time arrives, Madison Avenue is falling back -- on one of the most popular ploys in the marketing handbook, capitalizing on the calendar. By Stuart Elliott.  LIFESPAN-ECONSCENE (Undated) -- In extraordinary circumstances like this, President Bush said of the Terri Schiavo predicament, it is wisest to always err on the side of life. Uwe E. Reinhardt, a Princeton University health economist, said that from his perspective President Bush was not quite right: the president should have said err on the side of life years. By Alan B. Krueger.  SPORTS  (Coded s)  BKC-MICH-STATE (Kansas City) -- Last season, UConn pulled off the double, with the men's and women's basketball teams each winning their respective Division I championships, something no school had accomplished before. BKN-LAKERS (Los Angeles) -- The Lakers, without Phil Jackson and Shaquille O'Neal but with Kobe Bryant, now seem certain to miss out on the playoffs, one of the more remarkable developments in the 2004-05 NBA season.
