CIRCUITS  (Coded a)  Editors, Circuits stories in The New York Times have moved from their own free-standing section into the Thursday business section. The News Service, however, will continue to budget and distribute these stories in exactly the same way as before, using the same special selector code (tie-z) and the a category code (instead of the usual f for business stories).  In addition, The Times plans to publish special Circuits sections throughout the year, tentatively scheduling eight of them for the remainder of 2005. The News Service will budget and distribute these stories as well.  Two features will be absorbed into other sections and be budgeted and filed as part of these sections: GAME-THEORY becomes a part of the Weekend section and ONLINE-SHOPPING will go to a new weekly Style section. A third feature, DIGITAL-DIARY, is being dropped.  Circuits stories from The New York Times News Service for Thursday, March31.  CIR-CIRCUITS-COLUMN (Undated) -- The weekly State of the Art column: These days, a movie can reach you by pay-per-view, on a plane, at the video store, on a TV movie channel, on the Internet, at the front of a Greyhound bus, in the second row of a minivan or even -- get this -- in a movie theater. Not to seem ungrateful, but none of these delivery mechanisms deliver the holy grail: any movie, any time. There is, however, a low-tech distribution channel that comes pretty darned close: DVD rentals by mail. Seven years ago, Netflix invented the category, the business model and the prepaid, two-way shipping envelope. Now, as nearly 3 million subscribers are helping Netflix hit its profitable stride, Blockbuster and Wal-Mart have entered the market by taking a page from the Netflix playbook. Actually, they've taken pretty much the entire playbook. All three services look and work almost exactly alike. Netflix must be furious. By David Pogue.  CIR-CELLPHONE-FEATURES (Undated) -- In recent years, cellphone makers have tended to view their technologies that millions of people press to their faces every day less as simply phones and more as platforms for a plethora of services and features. Practically each new iteration of cellphone promises more, like digital music, streaming video, 3-D video games, location-based navigation and full Internet browsing -- not to mention a camera. And with more features often come more buttons, complications, costs and thicker operating manuals. Some critics call the phenomenon feature creep. And not all users are happy. By Michel Marriott and Katie Hafner.  CIR-Q&amp;A (Undated) -- Techno questions answered: What an RSS feed is; replacing the rechargeable battery on a Pocket PC; Internet security programs for Windows users. By J.D. Biersdorfer.  CIR-GEEK-NOTES (Undated) -- Techno news and notes:  -- All-in-one home entertainment center package for digital audio and video, for $10,000;  -- M-Audio Trigger Finger, a finger pad peripheral, lets you program and perform percussion and drum parts by drumming your fingers;  -- New cable lets you download photos from camera to photo iPod, bypassing the computer;  -- 3M's blackout privacy technology makes on-screen data visible only to the user; passersby see a black screen;  -- Voice Of The Fan allows ESPN site visitors to create an animated character, then record their rants and raves (about the NCAA basketball tournament, for example) and share them with the millions of others.  HOME  (Coded l)  KIDS-WING (Undated) -- When Ashlee Burt arrives home from school, she goes upstairs to her personal suite on the second floor of her family's spacious Spanish-style home in Saddle River, N.J., where a bedroom, bathroom and sitting room are decked out with all the creature comforts a 15-year old needs. Besides a computer, a television and DVD player, there is a private telephone line. Everything in the suite, including a convertible sofa, is decorated with her favorite jungle theme including zebra stripes and jungle foliage painted onto the walls and ceilings. A bamboo frame bed is covered with an animal print fabric. As the size of the average American house continues to grow, the simple kids room with bunk beds and shelves cluttered with toys and books is morphing into an entire wing or a suite. Like the master suites enjoyed by parents, these kids' quarters often come with a dressing area, a walk-in closet and a space for entertaining -- in this case for sleepovers. By Andree Brooks.  OBRIEN-FABRIC (New York) -- When Louis Comfort Tiffany built his dream estate on Long Island, in the early 1900s, he was so obsessed with the furnishings he did everything short of inventing the glue gun. For the dining room chairs he chose French velvet, dying it in a deep-blue-and-ivory pattern that echoed wisteria growing nearby. The house is long gone, but the Metropolitan Museum of Art plans to bring the dining room back to life for an exhibition next year, using photographs, salvaged fragments and the skills of Kevin O'Brien, a Philadelphia fabric designer, inventor and entrepreneur. O'Brien, 46, has been making unusual fabrics for more than a decade, attracting couturiers like Zac Posen; celebrities like Whoopi Goldberg and stores like Saks, which is carrying his spring line of linen, silk and velvet pillows. By Deborah Baldwin.  ST-LOUIS-PRESERVE (Undated) -- Until it was torn down last month, the neoclassical Century Building in St. Louis, with its 10-storey marble facades accented by ornate friezes and pilasters, graced an entire downtown city block. But after months of protests and lawsuits brought by historic preservationists, the building was razed in February to make way for a parking lot. With its familiar plot and cast of characters -- preservationists squaring off against developers and politicians -- the battle for the Century felt in many ways like a typical preservation dispute. Yet it also had an unusual twist: For what is believed to be the first time, the National Trust for Historic Preservation, the nation's most powerful preservation group, sided with the wreckers and helped finance the redevelopment project that led to the Century's demise. By Bradford McKee.  GARDEN-Q&amp;A (Undated) -- Garden questions answered. By Leslie Land.
